| Get exclusive member benefits & effect social change. Join Today<br/>A feeding tube is a device that’s inserted into your stomach through your abdomen. It’s used to supply nutrition when you have trouble eating. It’s also called percutaneous endoscopic gastrostomy (PEG), esophagogastroduodenoscopy (EGD), and G-tube insertion.<br/>This treatment is reserved for when you have trouble eating on your own. This can be because:<br/>The treatment can also done if you need it to receive certain medications.<br/>This procedure is performed in a hospital or clinic. Before you begin, tell your doctor about any medications you’re taking, including blood thinners such as warfarin (Coumadin), aspirin, or clopidogrel (Plavix). You’ll need to stop taking blood thinners or anti-inflammatory medications one week or so before the procedure. Your doctor will also need to know if you have certain conditions such as:<br/>If you have diabetes, your oral medications or insulin may have to be adjusted the day of the procedure.<br/>Your doctor will perform a gastrostomy using an endoscope, which is a flexible tube with a camera attached. You may be given anesthesia to make you more comfortable. This may make you drowsy following the procedure. Arrange before the procedure to have someone available to drive you home.<br/>This procedure requires you to fast. Typically, doctors ask that you abstain from eating eight hours before the procedure. Most people can return home the same day as the procedure or the following day.<br/>During the procedure, you’ll be asked to remove any jewelry or dentures. You’ll then be given an anesthetic and something to relieve the pain. You’ll lie on your back. Your doctor will place the endoscope in your mouth and down your esophagus. The camera will help the doctor visualize your stomach lining to ensure that the feeding tube is positioned properly.<br/>When your doctor can see your stomach, they’ll make a small incision in your abdomen. Next, they’ll insert the feeding tube through the opening. They will secure the tube and place a sterile dressing around the site. There may be a little drainage of bodily fluids, such as blood or pus, from the wound. The whole procedure usually lasts less than an hour.<br/>The feeding tube can be temporary or permanent, depending on the primary reason for the feeding tube.<br/>The medicine may make you drowsy. Plan on resting after the procedure. Your abdomen should heal in about five to seven days. After the tube is inserted, you may meet with a dietician who will show you how to use the tube for feeding. Your dietician will also educate you on how to care for the tube.<br/>Drainage around the tube is normal for a day or two, and a nurse will probably change your dressing on a regular basis. Feeling pain for a few days around the place where the incision was made is normal. Make sure to keep the area dry and clean to avoid skin irritation or infection.<br/>There are some risks associated with the procedure, but they aren’t common. Risks include trouble breathing and nausea from the medication. Excessive bleeding and infection are risks whenever you have surgery, even with a minor procedure such as a feeding tube insertion.<br/>Before you leave the hospital or clinic, make sure you know how to care for your feeding tube and when you need to contact a doctor. Go. | Hanki yksinomainen jäsenedut & vaikutus sosiaalinen muutos.<br/>Syöttöputki on laite, joka työnnetään vatsaan vatsan kautta, jota käytetään ravinnon hankkimiseen, kun on vaikeuksia syödä. Sitä kutsutaan myös perkutaaniseksi endoskooppiseksi gastrostomyksi (PEG), esofagogastrododenoskopiaksi (EGD) ja G-putken sisäänpanoksi.<br/>Tämä hoito on varattu silloin, kun sinulla on vaikeuksia syödä itse. Tämä voi johtua siitä, että:<br/>Hoito voidaan tehdä myös, jos tarvitset sitä tiettyjen lääkkeiden saamiseksi.<br/>Tämä menettely suoritetaan sairaalassa tai klinikalla. Ennen kuin aloitat, kerro lääkärillesi kaikista lääkkeistä, joita käytät, mukaan lukien verenohennuslääkkeet, kuten varfariini (Coumadin), aspiriini tai klopidogreeli (Plavix). Sinun on lopetettava verenohennuslääkkeiden tai tulehduskipulääkkeiden käyttö noin viikkoa ennen toimenpidettä. Lääkärin on myös tiedettävä, onko sinulla tiettyjä sairauksia, kuten:<br/>Jos sairastat diabetesta, suun kautta otettavia lääkkeitä tai insuliinia saatetaan joutua muuttamaan toimenpidepäivänä.<br/>Lääkäri suorittaa gastrostomian endoskoopilla, joka on joustava putki, johon on kiinnitetty kamera. Sinulle voidaan antaa anestesia, jotta olosi olisi mukavampi. Tämä voi tehdä sinut uneliaaksi toimenpiteen jälkeen. Järjestä ennen menettelyä, että joku voi ajaa sinut kotiin.<br/>Tämä menettely vaatii paastoamista. Tyypillisesti lääkärit pyytävät pidättäytymään syömisestä kahdeksan tuntia ennen toimenpidettä. Useimmat ihmiset voivat palata kotiin samana päivänä kuin menettely tai seuraavana päivänä.<br/>Toimenpiteen aikana sinua pyydetään poistamaan kaikki korut tai tekohampaat. Sitten sinulle annetaan puudutus ja jotain kivun lievittämiseksi. Makaat selälläsi. Lääkäri asettaa endoskoopin suuhusi ja ruokatorveesi. Kamera auttaa lääkäriä visualisoimaan mahalaukun limakalvon varmistaakseen, että syöttöputki on sijoitettu oikein.<br/>Kun lääkäri näkee vatsasi, he tekevät pienen viillon vatsaasi. Seuraavaksi he työntävät syöttöputken aukon läpi. He kiinnittävät putken ja asettavat steriilin sidoksen ympärille. Haavasta voi valua hieman elimistön nesteitä, kuten verta tai mätää, koko toimenpide kestää yleensä alle tunnin.<br/>Syöttöputki voi olla väliaikainen tai pysyvä, riippuen syöttöputken ensisijaisesta syystä.<br/>Lääke voi tehdä sinut uneliaaksi. Suunnittele lepääminen toimenpiteen jälkeen. Vatsasi pitäisi parantua noin viidestä seitsemään päivään. Kun putki on asetettu, saatat tavata ravitsemusterapeutin, joka näyttää, miten putkea käytetään ruokintaan. Ravitsemusterapeutti myös kouluttaa sinua siitä, miten hoitaa putkea.<br/>Putken ympärillä tapahtuva tyhjennys on normaalia päivän tai kahden ajan, ja hoitaja todennäköisesti vaihtaa sidoksen säännöllisesti. Kipu muutaman päivän ajan ympäri paikkaa, jossa viilto tehtiin, on normaalia. Varmista, että alue pysyy kuivana ja puhtaana ihoärsytyksen tai infektion välttämiseksi.<br/>Menettelyyn liittyy joitakin riskejä, mutta ne eivät ole yleisiä. Riskejä ovat hengitysvaikeudet ja pahoinvointi lääkityksestä. Liiallinen verenvuoto ja infektio ovat riskejä aina, kun olet leikkauksessa, jopa pienellä toimenpiteellä, kuten syöttöputken asettamisella.<br/>Ennen kuin lähdet sairaalasta tai klinikalta, varmista, että osaat hoitaa ruokintaletkuasi ja milloin sinun on otettava yhteys lääkäriin. Soita lääkärillesi, jos:<br/>Käsikirjoitus: Tricia Kinman<br/>Lääketieteellisesti tarkasteltu: Dec 07, 2015: Steven Kim, MD<br/>Syötä oireesi Oireentarkistajaamme, jotta saat selville oireidesi mahdolliset syyt.<br/>Syötä mikä tahansa reseptilääkkeiden luettelo ja katso, miten ne ovat vuorovaikutuksessa toistensa ja muiden aineiden kanssa.<br/>Anna sen väri- ja muototiedot, ja tämä työkalu auttaa sinua tunnistamaan sen.<br/>Etsi tietoa lääkkeiden yhteisvaikutuksista, sivuvaikutuksista ja muusta. |
| For the past couple of days, my class and I have been learning about, primary colours, secondary colours and tertiary Colours. We have also learn’t about warm and cool<br/>colours. First i'm going to be talking about primary colours...<br/>The primary colours are, blue, yellow and red. If you mix, two colours at the same time, you would get secondary colours. Primary colours are really important because they can make secondary colours.<br/>Secondary colours are, green, violet and orange. To make green you would have to add, blue + yellow together. To make violet you would have to add, blue + red together. Finally, to make orange you would have to add, red + yellow together.<br/>Tertiary colours are made up with a primary colour and a secondary colour together. The tertiary colours are, light green, dark green, dark purple, light purple, dark orange and gold. To make light green you mix, Yellow + green, it’ll make light green. To make, dark green you would have to add, blue + green. To make dark purple, you would need to mix, blue + violet. To make light purple, you would have to mix, red + violet. To make dark orange you have to mix, red + orange together. Finally, to make gold, you add orange and yellow together.<br/>I have been learning about a lot of colours, that I know what colours to mix, what colours are in the secondary colours, primary colours and tertiary colours. I hope you have learn't a lot about colours.<br/>HannahT signing out - Lovvee Yalll | Parin viime päivän ajan luokkani ja minä olemme oppineet perusväreistä, sekundääriväreistä ja tertiääriväreistä. Emme ole myöskään oppineet lämpimistä ja viileistä väreistä. Ensin aion puhua perusväreistä...<br/>Päävärit ovat sininen, keltainen ja punainen. Jos sekoitat kahta väriä yhtä aikaa, saat toissijaisia värejä. Päävärit ovat todella tärkeitä, koska ne voivat tehdä toissijaisia värejä.<br/>Toissijaisia värejä ovat vihreä, violetti ja oranssi. Vihreän tekemiseen olisi lisättävä sininen + keltainen yhdessä. Violetin tekemiseen olisi lisättävä sininen + punainen yhdessä. Lopuksi oranssin tekemiseen olisi lisättävä punainen + keltainen yhdessä.<br/>Tertiääriset värit muodostuvat ensisijaisesta ja toissijaisesta väristä yhdessä. Tertiääriset värit ovat, vaaleanvihreä, tummanvihreä, tummanvioletti, vaaleanvioletti, tumman oranssi ja kulta. Vaaleanvihreän sekoittaminen, keltainen + vihreä, tekee vaaleanvihreäksi. Tehdäksesi, tummanvihreän, sinun täytyy lisätä, sininen + vihreä. Tehdäksesi tumman violetin, sinun täytyy sekoittaa, sininen + violetti. Tehdäksesi vaalean violetin, sinun täytyy sekoittaa, punainen + violetti. Tehdäksesi tumman oranssin, sinun täytyy sekoittaa, punainen + oranssi yhdessä. Lopuksi kullan valmistamiseksi lisätään oranssi ja keltainen yhteen.<br/>Olen oppinut paljon väreistä, että tiedän mitä värejä sekoittaa, mitkä värit ovat toissijaisissa väreissä, pääväreissä ja tertiääriväreissä. | Roger Peters Copyright © 2005<br/>Complete template (Sonnet numbers)<br/>If Shakespeare wrote Twelfth Night as a vehicle for his Sonnet philosophy then<br/>the logical elements that make up the Sonnet structure should predominate.<br/>The primacy of Nature, the priority of female over male, the possibility of<br/>increase, and the dynamic of truth and beauty should be evident from the<br/>So when it is revealed in Act 1 scene 2 that at some time before Orsino’s<br/>opening speech a ship was wrecked in a storm at sea, Shakespeare has Nature<br/>initiate the comic drama. Shakespeare begins the philosophic examination<br/>of the inconsistencies of the biblical mythology implicit in his title Twelfth<br/>Night by first restoring Nature to its logical priority.<br/>Then, to correct the male-God presumption in beliefs based on the<br/>myths of old, Nature effects the separation of female and male. The<br/>biological twins Viola and her brother Sebastian undergo a virtual sexual<br/>division during the storm. In terms of the Sonnet logic, Viola and Sebastian<br/>represent the division within Nature (the sovereign mistress) into female (the<br/>28 Mistress sonnets) and the male (126 Master Mistress sonnets).<br/>After Viola and Sebastian find their way ashore at different locations, they<br/>remain separated until they are reunited in the final scenes of the play. But<br/>before Viola sets off inland Shakespeare shifts her sexual persona into the<br/>masculine. While Sebastian keeps his male identity, Viola forgoes her female<br/>identity and assumes the guise of a male. By converting Viola to a virtual<br/>male, Shakespeare is able to explore the consequences for natural logic when<br/>female and male roles are prejudicially reversed, as they are in beliefs that take<br/>literally the non-biological male/female priority in the Judeo/Christian<br/>Shakespeare’s intent, then, is to show that the Judeo/Christian inversion<br/>of the natural priority of female over male has drastic consequences for the<br/>dynamic of truth and beauty, or the logical dynamic of the mind. The<br/>inversion is given particular expression in the characters of Olivia and her<br/>manservant Malvolio. Their self-absorption and injudicious behaviour is the<br/>principal cause of distress and entertainment for the rest of the cast. The<br/>play’s drama takes their alienation from the natural logic and exposes it to<br/>the dynamic of truth and beauty Shakespeare articulates in the Sonnets.<br/>The play’s title signals Shakespeare’s intention to demonstrate the illogicality<br/>of biblical beliefs. The ‘Twelfth Night’ in the Christian calendar is<br/>the Feast of the Epiphany, which ‘celebrates the manifestation of the divinity<br/>of Christ to the Gentiles’(OED). Because Shakespeare’s Twelfth Night is based<br/>in Nature and sets out to examine the illogicalities of prioritising the male<br/>over the female, it critiques the inconsistencies in traditional beliefs.<br/>The or,What you will, which completes the title, points to the arbitrariness<br/>of the claim to divinity. Shakespeare sets out to examine with appropriate<br/>mock-seriousness the inconsistencies in the biblical claim, and its illogical<br/>consequences for human understanding and well-being.<br/>Shakespeare takes the theatergoer through a drama that deconstructs the<br/>inconsistencies of biblical faith and shows how they can be resolved in natural<br/>logic. He reveals the method by which he is able to take the logical elements<br/>of any mythological expression and reformulate them correctly to create a<br/>play of mythic depth based on a consistent philosophy.<br/>Not surprisingly, Twelfth Night, more so than most other plays, exercises<br/>a peculiar hold on the imaginations of commentators who on the one<br/>hand admire its comic verve but then invariably quibble endlessly about the<br/>deeds and fortunes of the characters. Because the Sonnet philosophy is not<br/>understood it is no wonder that commentators such as Harold Bloom and<br/>the critic he ‘worships’, Samuel Johnson, have misunderstood the play.<br/>From the vantage of the Sonnet logic, it is possible to say that the critics’<br/>fascination and criticism is driven by the play’s underlying challenge to the<br/>inconsistency of belief in a male God. As the Sonnets are based in Nature and<br/>not a system of arbitrary male-based belief, it is not surprising that Shakespeare<br/>has Nature provide the overarching context for Twelfth Night.<br/>Analysis of Twelfth Night<br/>Duke Orsino’s opening lines in Twelfth Night locate his personality between<br/>that of the mythic Poet and the idealising Master Mistress of the Sonnets.<br/>Shakespeare, as he does with Duke Vincentio in Measure for Measure, grounds<br/>his drama in a character who has some of the logical insights of the Poet<br/>but is still susceptible to the psychological idealising of the Master Mistress.<br/>Orsino’s first few lines recall the way the Poet characterises beauty and<br/>music in the Mistress sequence. His comments reveal his appreciation of the<br/>logic of music as a form of beauty or sensation. He understands that any<br/>sensation taken to an extreme leads to its opposite. Orsino recognises that<br/>his love for Olivia has been based in sensation, which sonnet 137 characterises<br/>as capable of passing from ‘best’ to ‘worst’ from one moment to the<br/>next without discrimination. And like the Poet in sonnet 130, Orsino appreciates<br/>the equivalence of his current ambivalence toward love and music with<br/>the primary sensations of taste, smell, hearing, touch and sight.<br/>If Music be the food of Love, play on,<br/>The introduction of music in the first line of the play recalls sonnets 8<br/>and 128, the two sonnets dedicated to music. In sonnet 8, particularly,<br/>Shakespeare is at pains to inculcate the youth into the logic of harmony or<br/>‘concord’. Concord is achieved when the youth realises that the combination<br/>of notes in music is analogous to the increase dynamic. The connection to<br/>natural logic would enable him to achieve the insights into the love he<br/>desires. But if music is played to excess its effect will ‘sicken and so die’ like<br/>odours that lose their sweetness.<br/>Give me the excess of it: that surfeiting,<br/>The appetite may sicken, and so die.<br/>That strain again, it had a dying fall:<br/>O, it came o’er my ear, like the sweet sound<br/>That breathes upon a bank of Violets;<br/>Stealing, and giving Odour. Enough, no more,<br/>’Tis not so sweet now, as it was before. (1.1.5-12)<br/>By characterising Orsino as a male who desires to mature from a lovestruck<br/>state, Shakespeare recalls the dilemma of the idealising Master<br/>Mistress. And by giving Orsino insights into beauty and truth and music<br/>that the Poet derives from the Mistress, Shakespeare introduces the logic of<br/>the Mistress sequence in the first lines of the play.<br/>The inability of editors to understand the logic of the Sonnets makes it<br/>impossible for them to appreciate the significance of the opening lines of<br/>the play. And their ignorance of Shakespeare’s philosophy is highlighted in<br/>the second half of Orsino’s speech where he compares the ‘spirit of Love’<br/>to the ‘Sea’. Revealingly the editors make a telling emendation to the<br/>punctuation in the first few lines.<br/>O spirit of Love, how quick and fresh art thou,<br/>Under the influence of Rowe from 1709, the full-stop after ‘Sea’ in the<br/>Folio has been altered to a comma, and the ‘Nought’ reduced to lower case.<br/>The commentators have preferred to read the passage as an extended characterisation<br/>of the ‘spirit of Love’. The emendation, though, kills the original<br/>meaning that characterises the ‘Sea’ or Nature as the contextualising priority<br/>for the play.<br/>That notwithstanding thy capacity,<br/>Receiveth as the Sea. Nought enters there,<br/>Of what validity, and pitch so ere,<br/>But falls into abatement, and low price<br/>Even in a minute; so full of shapes is fancy,<br/>That it alone, is high fantastical. (1.1.13-19)<br/>In the Sonnet logic, Nature is prior to the separation of female and male,<br/>and the logic of increase. Sonnet 9 in the increase group introduces love<br/>into the set conditional upon the acceptance of the logical relation of Nature<br/>and increase. Hence love is consequent upon Nature and all forms of love<br/>derive from the human potential for increase.<br/>With the Folio punctuation, the ‘spirit of Love’ is intentionally characterised<br/>as having less ‘capacity’ than the Sea, despite ‘Love’s’ ability to appear<br/>‘quick and fresh’. Then, according to the Folio punctuation, ‘Nought’ enters<br/>the Sea of whatever validity or pitch but encounters the priority of Nature.<br/>So, however ‘full of shapes’ is the ‘fancy’ that is love, the Sea or Nature ‘alone’<br/>is ‘high fantastical’. This reading is both consistent with the original punctuation<br/>and consistent with the role of the Sea as the initiating cause and<br/>contextualising entity of the drama.<br/>Rowe, and commentators since, have preferred to convert the passage<br/>so that it conforms to Judeo/Christian prejudices. When they hear ‘spirit<br/>of Love’ they alter the sense so that ‘spirit of Love’ seems ‘high fantastical’<br/>or God-like. If, for them, love is divine, and they need a Shakespeare sympathetic<br/>to Christianity, then their emendation right at the beginning of Twelfth<br/>Night engineers the sought-for reading. Ironically, they then continue to<br/>denigrate aspects of the play as has happened from the time of Samuel<br/>Johnson to the quibbles of his ‘worshiper’ Harold Bloom.<br/>And Shakespeare continues to introduce elements of his Sonnet<br/>philosophy. When Curio asks Orsino if he ‘will go hunt’ for ‘the Hart’,<br/>Orsino deliberately misinterprets the request as a question about the state of<br/>his heart. His pun on ‘Hart’ directs attention to the relation of the heart to the<br/>eyes in the Sonnets. Sonnet 14 identifies the ‘eyes’ as the source of truth and<br/>beauty, which give access to the heart. But the ‘eye’ is also the sexual eye or<br/>the eye through which desires are realised. So Shakespeare has Orsino<br/>combine the logic of the sexual and the erotic in his punning response.<br/>Why so I do, the Noblest that I have:<br/>The use of ‘mine eyes’ at this early stage in the drama continues the<br/>introduction of logical elements from the Sonnet philosophy. The association<br/>of the ‘eyes’ in sonnet 14 (and many other sonnets) with the logic of<br/>truth and beauty signals Shakespeare’s determination to examine the<br/>dynamic of truth and beauty throughout the play. The added combination<br/>of the sexual and the erotic in these first few lines establishes the mythic<br/>basis on which the comic drama will unfold. So within the space of 20 or<br/>so lines Shakespeare has introduced the logical elements that ensure his<br/>play will have a mythic depth independent of traditional mythologies or<br/>mythological entities, and free of the illogicalities associated with prioritising<br/>the biblical male God.<br/>O when mine eyes did see Olivia first,<br/>Me thought she purg’d the air of pestilence;<br/>That instant was I turn’d into a Hart,<br/>And my desires like fell and cruel hounds,<br/>Ere since pursue me. (1.1.23-8)<br/>But Shakespeare is not finished. When Valentine enters he reports that<br/>he was not admitted to the presence of the ‘Cloistress’ Olivia. Olivia, after<br/>her brother’s death, is determined to seclude herself from the forces of life.<br/>The play’s opening recalls Measure for Measure where Lord Angelo is the<br/>blind idealist ripe for a fall. But unlike Angelo, Orsino has been given some<br/>of the insights of the Poet of the Sonnets. For her part, Olivia the cloistress<br/>is like Isabella the novice nun whose worldly inexperience made it difficult<br/>for her to anticipate Angelo’s lust.<br/>So please my Lord, I might not be admitted,<br/>Shakespeare casts Olivia as a cloistress to critique both the illogicality of<br/>biblical belief, which prioritises the erotic over the sexual, and the illogical<br/>attitude toward death in idealist cultures. He contrasts the difference between<br/>Orsino, whose insights into the logic of myth are captured in his awareness<br/>of the logic of ‘mine eyes’, with Olivia’s ‘eye-offending brine’, which represents<br/>an inversion of the eye logic of the Sonnets. The psychological basis<br/>of Bible-based religions is epitomised in the lachrymal function of the eyes.<br/>But from her handmaid do return this answer:<br/>The Element it self, till seven years heat,<br/>Shall not behold her face at ample view:<br/>But like a Cloistress she will veiled walk,<br/>And water once a day her Chamber round<br/>With eye-offending brine: all this to season<br/>A brother’s dead love, which she would keep fresh<br/>And lasting, in her sad remembrance. (1.1.30-8)<br/>Compared with the ‘spirit of Love’, which is able to be ‘quick and fresh’,<br/>Olivia’s ‘brother’s dead love’ can be kept ‘fresh and lasting’ only in her ‘sad<br/>remembrance’. Not only do her eyes ‘offend’ natural logic, her morbid desire<br/>to keep a brother’s ‘dead love’ alive is symptomatic of the divine love kept<br/>alive by nuns in their cloisters. Shakespeare reiterates his understanding that<br/>love derives from the priority of sexual increase over the erotic mind, and<br/>not from the worship of an issueless resurrected male God. Commentators<br/>claim that ‘brother’s dead love’ is a quibble for the mutual love of brother<br/>and sister, but the wording is specific in its critique of unnatural love.<br/>Orsino ends the scene with a condemnation of the cloistered love Olivia<br/>wishes to offer her dead brother. He predicts that the consequence of such<br/>‘love’ for a dead brother will be the death of ‘all affections’ that ‘live in her’.<br/>O she that hath a heart of that fine frame<br/>Like the idealising and immature Master Mistress, Olivia has become full<br/>of self love, and because of the extent of her disease she will make her inner<br/>self her ‘king’. And because Orsino is sufficiently tuned to the Sonnet logic<br/>to not want such a fate for himself or for her, he turns to the fertility of<br/>Nature in ‘sweet beds of flowers’ where ‘love-thought’s lie rich’. He appreciates<br/>that only love based in Nature can give voice to thoughts that represent<br/>unprejudiced and unselfish love.<br/>To pay this debt of love but to a brother,<br/>How will she love, when the rich golden shaft<br/>Hath kill’d the flock of all affections else<br/>That live in her. When Liver, Brain, and Heart,<br/>These sovereign thrones, are all supplied and fill’d<br/>Her sweet perfections with one self king:<br/>Away before me, to sweet beds of Flowers,<br/>Love-thoughts lie rich, when canopied with bowers. (1.1.39-48)<br/>When in the second scene Viola, with the ship’s Captain and sailors,<br/>makes landfall on the beach in Illyria, Shakespeare establishes that the<br/>shipwreck and the separation of the twin brother and sister occurred before<br/>scene 1. Viola, at first wonders somewhat facetiously if her brother is in<br/>Elysium or heaven, if by chance he has not drowned. The Captain reminds<br/>her that it is by chance she was saved, and she hopes that her brother might<br/>also ‘perchance’ be saved. Shakespeare ties the element of chance to the<br/>separation and possible survival of brother and sister.<br/>Viola. And what should I do in Illyria?<br/>Viola’s acceptance of chance in Nature contrasts with the description<br/>of Olivia as a cloistered idealist, who desires to keep the idea of her dead<br/>brother alive. Viola’s role in the play, as the agent who liberates Olivia from<br/>her illogical state, and as the eventual sexual partner for Orsino, is<br/>consistent with Shakespeare’s logical insight into life and death articulated<br/>in the Sonnets.<br/>My brother he is in Elysium,<br/>Perchance he is not drowned: What think you sailors?<br/>Captain. It is perchance that you your self were saved.<br/>Viola. O my poor brother, and so perchance may he be.<br/>Captain. True Madam, and to comfort you with chance,<br/>Assure your self, after our ship did split,<br/>When you, and those poor number saved with you,<br/>Hung on our driving boat: I saw your brother<br/>Most provident in peril, bind himself,<br/>(Courage and hope both teaching him the practice)<br/>To a strong Mast, that liv’d upon the sea: (1.2.54-64)<br/>In the traditional commentaries on Twelfth Night, Orsino is frequently<br/>represented as a melancholic lover, but when scene 1 is read in the light<br/>of the Sonnet logic it can be seen that he is more insightful than melancholic.<br/>So when Viola asks the Captain ‘who governs Illyria’, he responds<br/>that Orsino is ‘a noble Duke in nature, as in name’. Viola recalls that her<br/>father mentioned him and that he was a ‘Bachelor’. When the Captain<br/>mentions that Orsino was courting Olivia, Viola retorts ‘What’s she?’ Her<br/>quick retort indicates a prior interest in Orsino that will be fulfilled at the<br/>The Captain describes Olivia as ‘virtuous maid’ who, because of the<br/>deaths of her father and brother has ‘abjur’d the sight and company of men’.<br/>So both Valentine and the Captain characterise Olivia as one who nun-like<br/>removes herself from the intercourse of men. The increase argument of the first 14<br/>sonnets enters the play through the agency of the canny Viola, and the final<br/>scenes of the play confirm the nature of her mission.<br/>But Shakespeare not only establishes Viola in the role of the Poet of the<br/>Sonnets who argues for the logic of increase to the recalcitrant Master<br/>Mistress. He also has Viola cross dress to parody the illogicality of the priority<br/>of the male God in biblical beliefs whose doctrines, contrary to natural logic,<br/>foster cloistering as a higher state of being. Significantly, Viola asks the<br/>Captain to disguise her not just as a male, but as a ‘Eunuch’ in a possible<br/>reference to the impotency of the neutered male God.<br/>Conceal me what I am, and be my aid,<br/>Viola’s contribution to the logic of the play is to critique the sexual<br/>inversion in the idealised male God and restore the logic of Nature. The<br/>Captain summarises the intentional deceit in a wonderful bit of Sonnet based<br/>paradox. If Viola is to be the Duke’s Eunuch, to parody his attraction to a<br/>cloistress, then the Captain will be Viola’s Muse to parody the lie they cannot<br/>speak. He realises that, ironically, if his ‘tongue blabs’, he will abjure the logic<br/>of truth and beauty that Viola will demonstrate through ‘mine eyes’.<br/>For such disguise as haply shall become<br/>The form of my intent. (1.2.105-13)<br/>Be you his Eunuch, and your Muse I’ll be,<br/>Scenes 1 and 2 of the first Act quite deliberately lay down the logical<br/>foundations of Shakespeare’s Sonnet philosophy and announce his intent to<br/>critique and restore the illogicalities associated with those who take biblical<br/>mythology literarily, as did the Christian sects of Shakespeare’s day.<br/>If there was any doubt that the two scenes establish the platform for the<br/>rest of the drama, Toby’s comment at the beginning of scene 3 restates its<br/>When my tongue blabs, then let mine eyes not see. When Maria questions Toby’s irregular behaviour, he<br/>intuitively objects to her strictures because he knows that being drunk is far<br/>less an offense than unnatural cloistering brought on by an illogical belief<br/>in death. (1.4.280-5)<br/>While Viola is unsure about the Orsino’s motives for testing Olivia, she<br/>is clear in her mind about her own feelings for him.<br/>I’ll do my best<br/>When Maria and Feste enter in scene 5, they continue the sexual<br/>innuendo of the previous scenes. Shakespeare is persistent in locating the<br/>logical basis of language in the sexual dynamic in Nature. Language gains<br/>its content from the natural logic of humankind as sexual beings, who must<br/>increase to persist. Shakespeare’s language is able to represent any circumstance<br/>between characters and events because it does not deny the logical<br/>basis of language in the sexual/erotic dynamic.<br/>To woo your Lady: yet a barful strife,<br/>Who ere I woo, my self would be his wife. (1.4.292-4)<br/>So when Maria challenges Feste’s absenteeism, he begins a repartee that<br/>relates being ‘well hung’ to his wittiness, the word wit itself being a pun on<br/>the sexual organs. Because Shakespeare has Feste acknowledge the sexual<br/>logic behind language (with his speech being figuratively well hung) then<br/>his language readily conveys his meaning despite his continual puns and<br/>ellipses. Maria’s observation that Feste’s ‘foolery’ is in his saying and not in<br/>his deeds neatly expresses Shakespeare’s intent. Feste acknowledges her<br/>insight by suggesting that as a ‘witty a piece of Eve’s flesh’ she would be a<br/>good match for Toby. Despite her somewhat dismissive response, Feste’s<br/>prediction becomes a reality by the play’s end.<br/>But Shakespeare’s intent is not merely to demonstrate the natural basis<br/>of language. As he does in other plays where characters engage in mock<br/>debates, he also intends to show the illogicality of traditional philosophical<br/>argument that prizes validity above soundness. The entry of Olivia and<br/>Malvolio, whose names anagrammatically suggest opposing instances of the<br/>same illogical attitude to life, incites Feste to give an example of a ‘simple<br/>Feste begins his exchange with Olivia by suggesting that a ‘witty’or wellhung<br/>‘fool’ is better than a ‘foolish’ or sexually confused ‘wit’ or vagina.<br/>Wit, and’t be thy will, put me into good fooling:<br/>Feste’s sardonic wish, ‘God bless thee Lady’, echoes his similarly irreverent<br/>call that ‘God give them wisdom that have it’ (1.5.330). Shakespeare<br/>demonstrates the illogicalities of the Judeo/Christian tradition by showing<br/>that the eroticism in its mythology acknowledges the logic of the sexual out<br/>of Nature, so God’s ‘blessing’ is a mixed one.<br/>those wits that think they have thee, do very oft prove<br/>fools: and I that am sure I lack thee, may pass for a<br/>wise man. For what says Quinapalus, Better a witty fool,<br/>than a foolish wit. God bless thee Lady. (1.5.326-30)<br/>Olivia’s response is to insist the ‘fool’ be taken away, but Feste retorts that<br/>as she is the fool she should go. When Olivia accuses him of being a ‘dry<br/>fool’ and ‘dishonest’, he counters with the mock syllogism. As he begins his<br/>argument he associates Olivia with the illogicality of belief in the efficacy<br/>of the virginity of the ‘Madonna’ or Mary the mother of God. Hence the<br/>sardonic irony in the idea that God bless ‘thee Lady’.<br/>Two faults Madonna, that drink and good counsel<br/>Feste begins by parodying the traditional uses of syllogisms, which derive<br/>a valid conclusion from false premises. The obvious unsoundness of Feste’s<br/>conclusion that a ‘dry fool’ and ‘a dishonest man mending himself ’ is ‘no<br/>longer dishonest’ is cause for humour. But his intention is to set up a more<br/>damning argument about traditional attitudes toward virtue and sin. Olivia’s<br/>(and the Mary the ‘Madonna’s’) so-called virtue of spiritualised celibacy is<br/>a sin against Nature. When such a sin seeks to amend or alleviate sorrow it<br/>is only ‘patched’ with a show of virtue. Feste’s mockery of traditional<br/>argument then leads to his sound conclusion. The ‘calamity’ of Olivia’s cloistering<br/>‘cuckolds’ Nature with worse consequences than for anyone who<br/>might cuckold a partner.<br/>will amend: for give the dry fool drink, then is the fool<br/>not dry: bid the dishonest man mend himself, if he mend,<br/>he is no longer dishonest; if he cannot, let the Botcher<br/>mend him: any thing that’s mended, is but patch’d: virtue<br/>that transgresses, is but patched with sin, and sin that<br/>amends, is but patched with virtue. If that this simple<br/>Syllogism will serve, so: if it will not, what remedy?<br/>As there is no true Cuckold but calamity, so beauty’s a&#124;<br/>flower; The Lady bad take away the fool, therefore I<br/>say again, take her away. (1.5.335-45)<br/>In his play titled Twelfth Night, or What you will, Shakespeare’s intent to<br/>critique traditional beliefs on the basis of the Sonnet philosophy has been<br/>laid down in the first few scenes. Already allusions have been made to cloistering,<br/>Christians, the Church, Mary, and the Madonna. As Shakespeare sees<br/>the whole play as a logical argument that establishes the soundness of his<br/>Nature based philosophy, it is not surprising that Feste’s mock argument<br/>should move toward the same conclusion.<br/>So neither is it surprising that when Feste continues his exchange with<br/>Olivia, and she brings her alter ego Malvolio into the debate, he persists in<br/>referring to Olivia as ‘Madonna’. Most commentators, wishing to defuse the<br/>critique of Church dogma, remove the capital M from Madonna, ironically<br/>making a proper name read as a descriptive noun. If there was any doubt<br/>that the butt of Feste’s previous ‘proof ’was Olivia’s decision to seclude herself<br/>from the society of men, it is removed in his offer to ‘prove’ Olivia a fool.<br/>Olivia. Sir, I bad them take away you.<br/>Feste begins by asserting that to ‘misprison him’ would be the ‘highest<br/>degree’ of error because it would be acting on a false belief. He encapsulates<br/>the nature of such belief in the Latin motto ‘Cucullus non facit<br/>monachum’, which translates as: ‘the hood does not make the monk’. Feste<br/>challenges Olivia’s desire to remain cloistered. He advises her against<br/>assuming such a practice because it leads to the ‘highest degree’ of error.<br/>Feste refuses to clutter his ‘brain’ with the mental burden of such religiosity.<br/>Feste. Misprison in the highest degree. Lady, Cucullus<br/>non facit monachum: that’s as much to say, as I wear not<br/>motley in my brain: good Madonna, give me leave to<br/>prove you a fool.<br/>Olivia. The more fool (Madonna) to mourn for your<br/>Olivia says she is willing to be proven a ‘fool’, but before Feste offers his<br/>formal ‘proof ’ he further clarifies his intent by advising her he will ‘catechize<br/>her’, or use her reliance on the simplistic doctrines of the Christian<br/>Catechism to catch her out. Olivia’s ‘virtue’ is mouse-like both because it<br/>is simplistic and because it undermines the natural virtue that Shakespeare<br/>articulates in the Sonnets.<br/>Then, with three simple questions, Feste proves that Olivia’s cloistered<br/>attitude is illogical even in the terms of her belief in heaven and hell. As all<br/>Christians presume their loved ones will be with them in heaven, because<br/>in their prejudice they reserve hell for non-believers or evil-doers such as<br/>murderers, then Olivia should have no need to mourn. If she did not believe<br/>in heaven and hell, she would have no need for such psychological refuge<br/>and would live life to the full, because in the natural logic of life, as sonnet<br/>144 argues, the heaven of one person can be another’s hell.<br/>Then when Malvolio speaks, his first words identify him as Olivia’s male<br/>persona. But because, unlike Olivia, he has completely suppressed his<br/>feminine sensibility he grossly manifests the effects of the religious prejudice<br/>from which Shakespeare will partly deliver Olivia by the play’s end. Malvolio,<br/>as the recalcitrant equivalent of the rival poets of the Sonnets, however,<br/>remains blindly conditioned by his selfish male ego. Like an unshakeable<br/>Christian who bases his understanding of life on the fear of death, Malvolio<br/>answers Olivia’s question about the logic of the ‘fools’ argument with the<br/>appropriate bad faith.<br/>Yes, and shall do, till the pangs of death shake<br/>And Feste responds to the hidden agenda in Malvolio’s remark by identifying<br/>God as the agent responsible for the life denying attitude.<br/>him: Infirmity that decays the wise, doth ever make the<br/>better fool. Malvolio’s egotistical<br/>defence of his prejudices sees him attribute his illogical attitudes to Feste,<br/>the same attitudes that are mocked mercilessly as the play progresses. With<br/>pointed irony, Shakespeare has Malvolio the fool accuse Feste the mock fool<br/>of needing a ‘minister’ to incite his wit. Twelfth Night critiques the twelfth day<br/>when the divinity of Christ was proclaimed by arguing against the death wish<br/>explicit in the Christian eschatology. What you will signals the ‘free disposition’<br/>available to those who do not take biblical mythologies literally.<br/>with a distemper’d appetite. What are you?<br/>Viola’s answer is framed to have the maximum effect on Olivia. By<br/>alluding to the way she was received by Toby and Malvolio, and by<br/>mentioning the issue of virginity most pertinent to Olivia’s state of mind,<br/>and by suggesting the matter involves divinity rather than profanation, she<br/>gets Olivia’s undivided attention. But Olivia had veiled her face when<br/>Viola/Cesario entered. In keeping with the Sonnet logic, and remembering<br/>that Olivia wept ‘eye-offending brine’ earlier, Viola’s task is to convince<br/>Olivia to lift the veil so that she can see her eye to eye.<br/>What would you?<br/>Viola. The rudeness that hath appeared in me, have I<br/>learn’d from my entertainment. What am I, and what I<br/>would, are as secret as maiden-head: to your ears,<br/>Divinity; to any others, profanation. (1.5.505-10)<br/>When Olivia relents and unveils to reveal her face and eyes to Viola/<br/>Cesario, Shakespeare uses Viola to re-establish for Olivia the logical precondition<br/>for truth and beauty. To sustain the illusion of divinity, Viola<br/>parodies Olivia’s divine conceit by exclaiming, ‘excellently done, if God did<br/>all’. But right at the moment of face-to-face or eye-to-eye engagement,<br/>Shakespeare moves from the patronising conditional of ‘if God’ to Viola’s<br/>statement of the basic logic of the Sonnet philosophy. Viola recites the logical<br/>conditions for Olivia’s beauty and for its persistence.<br/>Tis beauty truly blent, whose red and white,<br/>Viola’s short statement contains the whole of the Sonnet logic. In the plays<br/>and longer poems Shakespeare uses the contrast of red and white in cheeks<br/>or roses to symbolise the relation between the singularity of beauty and its<br/>relation to the oppositional logic of truth or language. So ‘beauty truly’<br/>follows on ‘Nature’s…cunning hand’ (read cunt), and beauty and truth<br/>cannot be sustained beyond the ‘grave’ except through increase or biological<br/>‘copying’. As Viola can now see Olivia’s eyes, she is able to begin returning<br/>Olivia to her natural balance.<br/>Nature’s own sweet, and cunning hand laid on:<br/>Lady, you are the cruel’st she alive,<br/>If you will lead these graces to the grave,<br/>And leave the world no copy. (1.5.547-8)<br/>Your Lord does know my mind, I cannot love him<br/>In the first scene, Orsino revealed an interest in Olivia that equates to<br/>that of the Poet of the Sonnets for the Master Mistress. (1.5.549-55)<br/>When Viola came ashore and adopted a male guise she imitated the role<br/>of God in biblical mythology. Viola finds herself in a double bind. Because of her male disguise,<br/>she cannot respond to Olivia’s infatuation with her pity-evoking female<br/>persona. And she cannot declare her love for Orsino while she remains<br/>disguised. Shakespeare captures the confusion that ensues when a female is<br/>cross-dressed as a male. He neatly depicts the illogicalities that arise when<br/>a male God usurps female Nature and so becomes impotent, and while he<br/>remains in the neutered state, is unable to resolve the psychology of<br/>stooping for, there it lies, in your eye: (2.2.670-1)<br/>The measure of Olivia’s infatuation is that, even though her eyes are now<br/>unveiled, she sees only Viola’s outer male guise instead of seeing into her<br/>eyes. In the Sonnet logic, the Poet is at pains to get the Master Mistress to<br/>appreciate the logic of the eyes from which he derives ‘truth and beauty’<br/>(sonnet 14). He criticises the rival poets whose focus on the outward<br/>appearance of the Master Mistress leads them to write verse with inferior<br/>content. Olivia, similarly, loses her ‘tongue’ because she misuses her eyes. If<br/>she could see clearly she would see through Viola’s disguise.<br/>I left no Ring with her: what means this Lady?<br/>Viola realises that Olivia has fallen in love with her male guise. Ironically,<br/>Olivia’s ‘cunning…passion’, or the desire driven by her ring/cunt, is elicited<br/>by a female dressed as a ‘man’. Shakespeare has Viola express a frequent<br/>refrain of the Sonnets that the ideal conceals within itself it own ‘wickedness’.<br/>She muses on the paradox that the male God’s ‘pregnant enemy’ is Nature,<br/>the usurped female.<br/>Fortune forbid my out-side have not charmed her:<br/>She made good view of me, indeed so much,<br/>That me thought her eyes had lost her tongue,<br/>For she did speak in starts distractedly. (2.2.673-7)<br/>She loves me sure, the cunning of her passion<br/>Viola reflects on the willingness with which some females conform to<br/>male demands. In Macbeth Shakespeare not only has Macbeth exercise his<br/>proud male persona in denial of his feminine side, Lady Macbeth, in her<br/>drive to align herself with her husband’s male God driven thirst for power,<br/>disavows her womanly capacity to suckle a child. Similarly in Twelfth Night,<br/>Olivia is seen as one who is susceptible to the unwarranted influence of her<br/>male persona. Viola distinguishes between the human ‘frailty’ of condoning<br/>excessive male pride within ‘women’s hearts’, and ‘we’, or females as ‘such’.<br/>Her role in the play is to demonstrate the way out of the male/male cul-de-<br/>Invites me in this churlish messenger:<br/>None of my Lord’s Ring? (2.2.678-84)<br/>How easy is it, for the proper false<br/>Because the commentators do not understand the logic, they make two<br/>telling emendations to the passage. The meaning instead<br/>is that some women are more susceptible to male/masculine guile and pride,<br/>but can resist it if they wish. The course of the drama bears out the logic<br/>articulated in the Sonnets.<br/>In women’s waxen hearts to set their forms:<br/>Alas, O frailty is the cause, not we,<br/>For such as we are made, if such we be: (2.2.685-8)<br/>Viola is already aware of the nature of Orsino’s love for Olivia. The<br/>precedent for her insight is established in sonnet 31 where the Poet warns<br/>the youth of the costly consequences of ‘dear religious love’. My master loves her dearly,<br/>The incongruity of the twisted gender relations in the biblical-based<br/>faiths of Shakespeare’s day, while patently obvious to his brilliant philosophic<br/>mind, must have made him reflect on the persistence of the iniquities of<br/>belief that work against natural logic and common sense. Viola expresses<br/>Shakespeare’s insight into the knottiness of the problem. And by the<br/>play’s end Shakespeare illustrates the difficulty by leaving Malvolio as the<br/>recalcitrant male who refuses to appreciate natural logic. (2.2.689-95)<br/>O time, thou must untangle this, not I,<br/>When Toby, Andrew and Feste again swap humorous jibes in scene 3 in<br/>which Toby mentions ‘the four Elements’, Shakespeare’s determination to<br/>parody the formalism in Aristotle’s syllogistic logic is apparent. Shakespeare<br/>mentions Aristotle twice in his works but fails to mention Plato, suggesting<br/>an empathy with Aristotle’s Nature based philosophy and a dismissal of<br/>Plato’s idealist realism.<br/>It is too hard a knot for me t’untie. (2.2.676-7)<br/>The Sonnet philosophy is precise in recognising Nature as prior to all else,<br/>and the youth sequence is devastating in its critique of the dangers of<br/>excessive idealism. But aspects of Aristotle’s logic remained under the<br/>influence of Plato’s idealised forms. The degree of abstraction in his logical<br/>relations and the organisation of his metaphysics into the four elements is<br/>the butt of Shakespeare’s wit (see sonnets 45/46).<br/>Toby. (2.3.700-12)<br/>Toby and Andrew’s ‘arguments’ are absurd because any argument based<br/>on unsound premises is absurd. It laughs at the religious idea that ‘love’ is in the hereafter or God.<br/>The words and the argument of the song are so faithful to the logic of the<br/>increase argument of the first 14 sonnets that only Shakespeare could have<br/>arranged its lyrics.<br/>O stay and hear, your true loves coming,<br/>That can sing both high and low.<br/>Trip no further pretty sweeting.<br/>Journey’s end in lover’s meeting,<br/>Every wise man’s son doth know.<br/>What is love, tis not hereafter,<br/>Present mirth, hath present laughter:<br/>What’s to come, is still unsure.<br/>In delay there lies no plenty,<br/>Then come kiss me sweet and twenty:<br/>Youth’s a stuff will not endure. (2.3.739-52)<br/>The singing of the song of increase immediately after the mockery of<br/>‘faith’, and Aristotelian logic and metaphysics, confirms Shakespeare’s intent<br/>to direct the characters in the drama away from untenable conceits so that<br/>they can recover their natural logic. Comic characters such as Toby, Andrew<br/>and Feste serve to anchor the argument of the play in the realities of life<br/>and ultimately in the priority of Nature and increase over the hereafter of<br/>God. (2.3.833-45)<br/>Shakespeare begins scene 4, as he did the first scene of the play, with<br/>Orsino calling for ‘Music’ in the form of a song to counter the effect of his<br/>‘love’ for Olivia. Again, Shakespeare relates Orsino’s love for Olivia to the<br/>unthinking sensations evoked by instrumental music. Orsino is aware that<br/>his emotional response to Olivia’s idealistic seclusion incites an intense but<br/>untenable sensation of love. His overheated desire for Olivia requires more<br/>than instrumental music if it is to be allayed permanently.<br/>Not surprising, then, Orsino asks to hear Feste sing ‘that piece of song’<br/>he heard the night before, which ‘did relieve my passion much’. He specifically<br/>acknowledges that the ‘verse’ or words of the song would be more<br/>effective than the ‘light airs’ of the instrumentalists.<br/>Give me some Music;Now good morrow friends.<br/>As Feste could not immediately be found to sing the ‘verse’, Orsino<br/>suggests the musicians ‘play the tune’ until the songster is located. He calls<br/>Viola/Cesario near to give her an account of his understanding of love.<br/>Shakespeare has Orsino express the logic of love he articulates in the Sonnets,<br/>and in particular the 14 increase sonnets. Orsino wishes to explain that his<br/>love for Olivia is not based in ‘unstaid and skittish’ emotions (of which the<br/>Poet accuses the Master Mistress) but in the ‘constant image of the creature’<br/>who is the ‘beloved’. His attraction to Olivia, like that of the Poet for the<br/>Master Mistress, is based more on a desire to correct her disposition, which<br/>tends to tears and celibacy.<br/>Now good Cesario, but that piece of song,<br/>That old and Antique song we heard last night;<br/>Me thought it did relieve my passion much,<br/>More than light airs, and recollected terms<br/>Of these most brisk, and giddy-paced times.<br/>Come, but one verse. (2.4.844-90)<br/>Come hither Boy, if ever thou shalt love<br/>Orsino’s use of the word ‘Boy’, and his reference to the beloved as a<br/>‘creature’, can only be understood in terms of the logic of the increase<br/>sonnets. The first line of sonnet 1 states that ‘From fairest creature we desire<br/>increase’, and the Master Mistress is referred to as ‘Boy’ 3 times in the set<br/>but particularly in sonnet 126 where the logic of increase is brought to a<br/>head. So Orsino, in keeping with his insights into Nature as the ‘high fantastical’<br/>sea in the first scene, accounts for his love of Olivia out of the constant<br/>logic of Nature and the consequence that logically creatures need to increase.<br/>In the sweet pangs of it, remember me:<br/>For such as I am, all true Lovers are,<br/>Unstaid and skittish in all motions else,<br/>Save in the constant image of the creature<br/>That is belov’d. (2.4.899-904)<br/>So when Orsino asks Viola/Cesario how she liked ‘this tune’, her answer<br/>matches his insight into the logic of love. Viola appreciates that instrumental<br/>music can only echo the emotions associated with the ‘seat of love’ or the<br/>heart. She knows that the sensations evoked by such music differ markedly<br/>from the articulation of thoughts through song, verse, or prose. Viola<br/>exhibits an awareness of the logic of beauty and truth presented in the<br/>Mistress sequence of the Sonnets. And Orsino acknowledges her ‘masterly’<br/>understanding by aligning it with ‘thine eye’.<br/>Viola. It gives a very echo to the seat<br/>While Orsino is focused on his mission to free Olivia from the grip of<br/>her cloistering temperament, he has not noticed that Viola/Cesario has<br/>recognised in him the temperament of the Sonnet Poet, a temperament very<br/>sympathetic to her own. So when Orsino questions her as to the source of<br/>her insights, the exchange is at cross purposes and for Viola beside the point.<br/>Orsino’s use of Sonnet imagery such as ‘Roses’ and Viola’s reference to the<br/>conditions of ‘dying’ and ‘growing’ indicate their common ground.<br/>Where love is throned.<br/>Orsino. Thou dost speak masterly,<br/>My life upon’t, young though thou art, thine eye<br/>Hath staid upon some favour that it loves:<br/>Hath it not boy? (2.4.905-10)<br/>The temporary offset between Orsino and Viola, though, is relieved by<br/>the arrival of Feste. Orsino informs Viola that the song to be sung is ‘old<br/>and plain’. His comment is reminiscent of the Sonnet philosophy where in<br/>sonnet 76 the Poet says his poetry is ‘far from variation or quick change’.<br/>Come away, come away death,<br/>Orsino’s preference for a song that ‘did relieve my passion much’ is<br/>evident in its unsentimental attitude to death. To counter death due to the<br/>‘fair cruel maid’, the song regards death as an event that no one else can<br/>‘share’. Only then, when there is no grave for the likes of Olivia to ‘weep<br/>on’, might she recognise death as part of life and not an end in itself. The<br/>song expresses the Sonnet understanding of life and death. In the increase<br/>sonnets life persists through increase, while death is but an event in the<br/>dynamic of life across generations. No wonder Orsino feels comfort at Feste’s<br/>song that gives ‘music’ a significance the Poet argues for in sonnet 8.<br/>And in sad cypress let me be laid.<br/>Fie away, fie away breath,<br/>I am slain by a fair cruel maid:<br/>My shroud of white, stuck all with Yew, O prepare it.<br/>My part of death no one so true did share it.<br/>Not a flower, not a flower sweet<br/>On my black coffin, let there be strewn:<br/>Not a friend, not a friend greet<br/>My poor corpse, where my bones shall be thrown:<br/>A thousand thousand sighs to save, lay me o where<br/>Sad true lover never find my grave, to weep there. (2.4.941-52)<br/>Before Feste departs, he sardonically identifies the source of Olivia’s<br/>melancholy with the God of religion who usurps the role of Nature, represented<br/>in Twelfth Night by the ‘Sea’. Those ‘men’ who lack ‘constancy’<br/>because they are melancholic over death, and have faith in the God of death,<br/>should be ‘put to Sea’. Their idealistic ambitions, when faced with the<br/>impossibility of having ‘everything’ and ‘everywhere’, might then accommodate<br/>life (‘it’), which provides a ‘good voyage’ through wanting ‘nothing’<br/>of such a God. (Commentators who identify Jupiter as the ‘melancholy God’<br/>avoid Shakespeare’s critique of the death psychology inherent in the biblical<br/>theology that promises a better ‘life’ in heaven, after preparing for that better<br/>‘life’ through celibacy or worldly denial.)<br/>Now the melancholy God protect thee, and the<br/>Orsino corroborates Feste’s intent when he describes Olivia to Viola/<br/>Cesario as a ‘sovereign cruelty’. He, though, is not attracted to her worldly<br/>‘Fortune’ but, paradoxically, his ‘soul’ is drawn to the ‘miracle’ of life that<br/>Nature has provided through her potential to increase.<br/>Tailor make thy doublet of changeable Taffata, for thy<br/>mind is a very Opal. I would have men of such constancy<br/>put to Sea, that their business might be every thing,<br/>and their intent every where, for that’s it, that always<br/>makes a good voyage of nothing. (2.4.959-64)<br/>Get thee to yond same sovereign cruelty:<br/>While Orsino reveals insights into the natural logic behind his love for<br/>Olivia, Viola/Cesario’s role is to teach him how to graduate to the next stage<br/>of listening. (2.4.980-90)<br/>Too well what love women to men may owe:<br/>When Orsino asks a question of Viola’s ‘history’, he reveals his incapacity<br/>to appreciate her challenge to his lack of awareness of his feminine persona.<br/>Shakespeare anticipates the academics who will write volumes of speculation<br/>about his biographical details, but who miss the simple logical insights into<br/>the female/male and feminine/masculine relationships that permeate his<br/>works. A male is no<br/>more than show if he cannot appreciate the implications of not developing<br/>his feminine persona. The invention of God should not lead to male<br/>hegemony but rather assist the male reconcile himself with the priority of<br/>the female within high fantastical Nature.<br/>But let concealment like a worm i’th bud<br/>Feed on her damask cheek: she pined in thought,<br/>And with a green and yellow melancholy,<br/>She sat like Patience on a Monument,<br/>Smiling at grief. Was not this love indeed?<br/>We men say more, swear more, but indeed<br/>Our shows are more than will: for still we prove<br/>Much in our vows, but little in our love. (2.4.999-1007)<br/>In scene 5 Shakespeare shows just how gullible the immature masculine<br/>mind is before the written word. Malvolio is so beguiled by his own idealised<br/>self-image that he will accept without question a letter otherwise intended<br/>to challenge his conceits. The written promise that his desires will be fulfilled<br/>is enough to convince him of their imminent realisation. In the critique of<br/>Malvolio’s impressionable mind, Shakespeare parodies those who believe<br/>literally in biblical mythology.<br/>Shakespeare’s critique of blind faith in the written word, and particularly<br/>Christian dogma, is addressed in the first few lines of the scene. When<br/>Toby encourages Fabian to ‘come thy ways’, Fabian replies in imagery that<br/>is reminiscent of the Christian attitude to death and its sanctification of<br/>Nay I’ll come: if I loose a scruple of this sport,<br/>In just a few words Shakespeare conveys the complex relation between<br/>the melancholy love of death and the valorisation of painful death as the<br/>ultimate expression of faith. Toby’s response is even more direct in its identification<br/>of Malvolio’s male-based self regard with the worse consequences<br/>of male-based faith. Using the word ‘niggard’ from the first sonnet, with its<br/>meaning of miserliness in respect to the logic of increase, Shakespeare<br/>characterises Malvolio as ‘Rascally sheep-biter’. Recalling that elsewhere<br/>Shakespeare alludes to the anagrammatic relation between God and Dog,<br/>and that Christ is the ‘shepherd’ of the flock, the phrase could identify<br/>Malvolio’s recalcitrance with a niggardly male Christ who plays God/Dog<br/>to keep his flock in order.<br/>let me be boiled to death with Melancholy. Commentators wonder that his part might have<br/>been meant for Feste, but Feste would not have been as delighted as Fabian<br/>to see Malvolio bested.<br/>Rascally sheep-biter, come by some notable shame? (2.5.1029-30)<br/>When Malvolio sights the letter, Shakespeare immediately takes the piss<br/>out of him by having him note the three letters ‘C’, ‘U’ and ‘T’ and the<br/>‘great P’s’. As his fantasies are dictated by his lust for Olivia’s ‘cunt’ he unwittingly<br/>spells out the letters for ‘cut’ or the female organ, and identifies its<br/>non-sexual function as the outlet for urine or ‘pee’.<br/>Ironically Olivia’s sealing wax bears the impress of Lucrece, the heroine<br/>of the Lucrece, whose idealised innocence left her unprepared for the<br/>compromise of her virtue by her husband and the consequent rape by<br/>Tarquin. Shakespeare identifies Olivia with Lucrece because she does not<br/>appreciate the logic of increase in Nature and its implication for the correct<br/>understanding of beauty and truth. Maria’s letter reinforces the allusion.<br/>I may command where I adore, but silence like a<br/>As Malvolio reads the letter he is so convinced that his fate lies with<br/>Olivia he will dress and behave in a way bound to offend her. (2.5.1114-7)<br/>The commentators make two unwarranted emendations. They change<br/>‘stallion’ to ‘staniel’ so killing the allusion to the winged Pegasus as symptomatic<br/>of Malvolio’s idealistic fantasies, and they change ‘become’ to ‘born’<br/>ironically using a word related to increase when the meaning is based on<br/>the ‘stars’ that sonnet 14 slights as a source of truth and beauty. Malvolio<br/>wants to ‘become’ great despite the logic of his birth and death.<br/>Scene 5 also establishes the basis for the eventual marriage of Toby and<br/>Maria. In its last few lines Toby recognises (echoed by Andrew) the qualities<br/>of a woman who knows, as a ‘most excellent devil of wit’, how to ‘lie’ to<br/>reveal the truth. In sonnet 138, the first of the truth sonnets in the Mistress<br/>sequence, the Poet and the Mistress ‘lie’ to each other to reveal the depth<br/>of their love. Unlike Malvolio and biblical literalists, they appreciate the logic<br/>of words. As Shakespeare knew, if myth is taken to be true it is a lie and if<br/>it is accepted as a lie it is true.<br/>Shakespeare’s understanding of the logic of truth and beauty in the<br/>Sonnets, and particularly the exposition of beauty and then truth in the<br/>Mistress sequence, is critical for an appreciation of the plays. The characters in<br/>the plays represent argument places in his critique of traditional beliefs and<br/>for the recovery of natural logic. Immediately after the gulling of Malvolio’s<br/>overwrought hopes, Shakespeare demonstrates through an exchange between<br/>his two most Sonnet aware characters the logic of truth or the possibilities of<br/>In the first scene of Act 3, Shakespeare emphasises that the lesson delivered<br/>through the idealistic stupidity of Malvolio when confronted with<br/>words is symptomatic of the psychology of belief exemplified by the Church’s<br/>blind faith in the wording of the Bible. Appropriately Viola and Feste present<br/>the demonstration of the logic of words. Viola has established herself as the<br/>most insightful character in the comic drama, and Feste, as the clown or fool,<br/>has demonstrated insight into the psychology of the other players.<br/>Viola enters and greets Feste with a challenge that associates God and<br/>Music. Her ‘‘Save thee’ is a contraction of ‘God save thee’ and her allusion<br/>to instrumental Music begins the exchange by pointing to the form of<br/>sensation or beauty generated in the mind through the intuitive combination<br/>of ideas. The idea of God and the artificiality of Music excite the mind to<br/>intensified internal sensations consequent on the logic of words and ordinary<br/>sensations derived through the senses from Nature.<br/>Feste responds not to Viola’s question about his means of livelihood,<br/>but to the status of God and Music. He differentiates between where he<br/>‘lives’ and the role of the Church.<br/>Viola. ’Save thee Friend and thy Music: dost thou live<br/>Viola extends the differentiation to include Kings, effectively identifying<br/>Church and Monarchy as institutions that ‘stand by’ indifferently toward the<br/>processes of everyday life.<br/>by thy Tabor?<br/>Feste. (3.1.1221-3)<br/>Feste. You have said sir:To see this age: A sentence is<br/>In the Sonnet logic, truth and beauty are not self-subsistent. Sensation<br/>and language derive their logic from the priority of Nature and the sexual<br/>dynamic. The whole system hangs together to provide for the possibility<br/>of acting and thinking in accord with Nature. When Churches and Kings<br/>claim the divine right to supplant Nature and give God priority over<br/>Nature, then their language is in conflict with natural logic. Feste not only<br/>identifies the conditions that give words their meaning, he implicates the<br/>‘Rascals’ like Christ and religious institutions that ‘bond’ words to<br/>unredeemable and unnatural expectations epitomised by the association of<br/>‘Word’ and ‘God’.<br/>but a chev’rel glove to a good wit, how quickly the<br/>wrong side may be turned outward.<br/>Viola. Nay that’s certain: they that dally nicely with<br/>words, may quickly make them wanton.<br/>Feste. I would therefore my sister had no name Sir.<br/>Viola. Why man?<br/>Feste. Why sir, her name’s a word, and to dally with<br/>that word,might make my sister wanton: But indeed,<br/>words are very Rascals, since bonds disgraced them. (3.1.1224-33)<br/>The mockery of traditional argument in the play reaches its pitch when<br/>Viola asks Feste for his ‘reason’. Reason alone or the juggling of words does<br/>not provide meaning for words. Together Viola and Feste play on the sexual<br/>basis of language using words like ‘nothing’ or the female sex organ to<br/>indicate the source of ‘reason’.<br/>Viola. Thy reason man?<br/>Feste is sufficiently aware of the logic of increase to know that anyone<br/>who marries merely for the bond of marriage alone is a ‘fool’. As a<br/>‘corruptor of words’, Feste counters the tendency for words like God to<br/>assume unwarranted meaning. To protect against such foolery, the interrelation<br/>between Master and Mistress should be encouraged, and Feste<br/>alludes to the source of Viola’s ‘wisdom’ in her successful combination of<br/>the feminine/masculine dynamic.<br/>Feste. (3.4.1649-50)<br/>Shakespeare contrasts the deep religious delusion sustained by Malvolio<br/>with the romantic fantasies of Sir Andrew Aguecheek. If Malvolio is<br/>susceptible to believe what he reads because of excessive self-regard, as do<br/>Christians, Andrew is unable to write what he thinks because he does not<br/>know what to believe about his chances with Olivia. The doubts Olivia has about the ‘male’ with a<br/>‘heart of stone’ are the same doubts believers have of God when he fails to<br/>respond to their emotional needs. It is then that God is seen as the Devil.<br/>But because Shakespeare’s cross-dressed female has adopted her role to<br/>prosecute his intent, she is able by the end of the play to liberate both Olivia<br/>and Orsino from the effects of Olivia’s God/death morbidity.<br/>A Fiend like thee might bear my soul to hell. This youth that you see here,<br/>The irony is profound. If Antonio had confronted the real Sebastian with<br/>a deed of ingratitude, the evil of the deed would be measured against the<br/>quiddity of the male. But instead he confronts Viola/Cesario, the crossdressed<br/>female, and so Shakespeare directs his attack on the cross-dressed<br/>male idol God who usurps the priority of Nature. And Shakespeare is<br/>insistent that the fault does not devolve upon Nature at large, but like the<br/>conceit of biblical belief, is a fault of the mind alone. The theme pervades<br/>Shakespeare’s works and is given precise expression in his long poem of 1593,<br/>Lucrece. There he says,<br/>I snatch’d one half out of the jaws of death,<br/>Reliev’d him with such sanctity of love;<br/>And to his image, which me thought did promise<br/>Most venerable worth, did I devotion.<br/>But oh, how vild an idol proves this God:<br/>Thou hast Sebastian done good feature, shame.<br/>In Nature, there’s no blemish but the mind:<br/>None can be call’d deformed, but the unkind.<br/>Virtue is beauty, but the beauteous evil<br/>Are empty trunks, o’er-flourish’d by the devil. Shakespeare has,<br/>from the beginning, indicated that Olivia and Sebastian have temperaments<br/>not sufficiently circumspect to see into the ‘eyes’ that reveal the<br/>natural logic of truth and beauty. Their melancholic dispositions are more<br/>suited to Church bound succour. Fittingly, in Twelfth Night, Olivia and<br/>Sebastian are married at the end of scene 4 so that Viola and the Duke are<br/>free to commit to each other without the psychological constructs of faith<br/>and Church at the play’s end.<br/>Plight me the full assurance of your faith,<br/>That my most jealous, and too doubtful soul<br/>May live at peace. (4.3.2140-3)<br/>At the beginning of Act 5, after Fabian’s play on the word ‘dog’ in<br/>response to Feste’s unwillingness to show him Malvolio’s ‘Letter’, Feste gives<br/>Orsino a lesson in the logic of truth consistent with that presented in the<br/>Mistress sequence of the Sonnets. To Orsino’s enquiry as to how he ‘does’,<br/>Feste replies with a seeming paradox.<br/>Feste. Truly sir, the better for my foes, and the worse<br/>While Orsino is aware of the priority of ‘high fantastical’ Nature over<br/>other fancies, he is not proficient in the logic of truth and beauty. He is<br/>drawn to Olivia more for her ignorance of its logic than by his command<br/>of truth and beauty consequent upon the logic. As the time nears for Viola<br/>to abandon her cross-dress disguise, Orsino first needs a lesson in the<br/>dynamic of truth and beauty. He needs to understand that the dynamic of<br/>true and false is not determined by social conventions but is apparent only<br/>in its logical relation with Nature. If Orsino were fully in command of his<br/>mind he would have already recognised his soul mate in the guise of Cesario.<br/>for my friends.<br/>Orsino. Just the contrary: the better for thy friends.<br/>Feste. No sir, the worse.<br/>Orsino. How can that be? (5.1.2165-9)<br/>Marry sir, they praise me, and make an ass of me,<br/>Feste treats Orsino to a lessen in pure natural logic. Orsino is struck as<br/>if by a revelation and responds, ‘this is excellent’. Then in a word-play on<br/>the sub-meanings of coin, Feste obliquely advises Orsino to start thinking<br/>about having sex with Viola.<br/>now my foes tell me plainly, I am an Ass: so that by my<br/>foes sir, I profit in the knowledge of my self, and by my<br/>friends I am abused: so that conclusions as to be kisses, if<br/>your four negatives make your two affirmatives, why<br/>then the worse for my friends, and the better for my foes. (5.1.2170-5)<br/>Put your grace in your pocket sir, for this once,<br/>He suggests Orsino has a ‘good play’ and that he should let his ‘bounty<br/>take a nap’ until he returns to ‘wake it’.<br/>And let your flesh and blood obey it. (5.1.2183-4)<br/>When Antonio re-enters with the officers, Orsino and the first Officer<br/>recognise him as a ‘pirate’ who fought at ‘sea’. Antonio’s role as the ‘saltwater<br/>Thief ’ rescuer of Sebastian makes him the intermediary between<br/>Nature and the others who are in varying degrees divorced from their natural<br/>logic. Viola intervenes to defend Antonio for his defence of her at the duel,<br/>and he insists he was not a ‘Thief, or Pirate’ but agrees he was Orsino’s<br/>enemy. Instead he describes his experiences in terms consistent with the<br/>A witchcraft drew me hither:<br/>In Macbeth, the Witches who open the play are the female element<br/>introduced after the thunder of Nature. Their role is confirmed when the<br/>license they take with the immature idealist Macbeth is later moderated by<br/>the more mature Hecat who restores the balance of the Sonnet logic. So<br/>Antonio’s recognition that ‘witchcraft’ drew him ‘here’ acknowledges the<br/>logical priority of the female over the male. His accusation that Viola as<br/>Cesario is an ungrateful ‘boy’ for rejecting his ‘love’, although he mistakes her<br/>for Sebastian, makes the logical point that any male who tries to ignore the<br/>consequence of enraging the ‘rude seas’ (or Nature) can still incur her ‘wrack’<br/>to end ‘life’ at any time.<br/>That most ingrateful boy there by your side,<br/>From the rude seas enrag’d and foamy mouth<br/>Did I redeem: a wrack past hope he was:<br/>His life I gave him, and did thereto add<br/>My love without retention, or restraint,<br/>All in his dedication. (5.1.2228-34)<br/>The words ‘boy’ and ‘wrack’ and the theme of Antonio’s concern recall<br/>sonnet 126, the final sonnet of the Master Mistress sequence. Antonio’s role<br/>as a ‘redeemer’ of ‘life’ is in contrast to Christ’s role as the symbol for death<br/>as life in heaven. And the generality of Antonio’s point is captured in his<br/>intuition that Viola acts with ‘false cunning’. In the Sonnets, and in plays like<br/>Macbeth, both the male and the masculine persona of the female is in danger<br/>of Nature’s wrack. Most editors change ‘wrack’ to ‘wreck’ because they do<br/>not appreciate the intent of the Sonnet logic.<br/>When Olivia enters she mistakes Viola for Sebastian and expects she/he<br/>will honour their marriage. And when Viola suggests she speak to Orsino<br/>she rates his words ‘as howling after Music’, recalling the earlier characterisation<br/>of Music as undifferentiated sensation. But then Orsino identifies his<br/>regard for Olivia as ‘devotion’ of his ‘soul’ to her ‘Altars’, confirming that<br/>her appeal has been more for her cloistered unassailability than for her flesh<br/>What to perverseness? You uncivil Lady<br/>Olivia appreciates that whatever ‘pleases’ Orsino suits him, and for his<br/>part, he realises he cannot redeem Olivia. He wonders if he should ‘kill’ the<br/>‘savage jealousy’ of ‘what I love’ because he ‘knows the instrument’ which<br/>partly keeps him from Olivia’s ‘Marble-breasted’ tyranny. Ironically he<br/>threatens to take Cesario away because he realises that Olivia ‘loves’ him.<br/>Yet Viola/Cesario is neither the man Olivia can love, nor does Orsino yet<br/>see that he is most suited to love Viola.<br/>To whose ingrate, and inauspicious Altars<br/>My soul the faithfull’st offerings have breath’d out<br/>That ere devotion tender’d. (5.1.2268-71)<br/>But this your Minion, whom I know you love,<br/>To Orsino, Olivia’s ‘cruel eye’ seems incapable of love. Shakespeare has<br/>Orsino identify Olivia with the ‘Lamb’ or Christ who she imitates in her<br/>flight from life to death. And Orsino senses the ‘Raven’ darkness in Olivia’s<br/>innocent ‘Dove’, just as Malvolio’s apparent goodness equally warrants his<br/>confinement in the dark.<br/>And whom, by heaven I swear, I tender dearly.<br/>Him I will tear out of that cruel eye,<br/>Where he sits crowned in his master’s spite. (5.1.2281-4)<br/>Come boy with me, my thoughts are ripe in mischief:<br/>Viola is more than happy to be offered an opportunity to be the focus<br/>of Orsino’s jealousy. She openly declares her love and preparedness to be<br/>his ‘wife’. But, despite Viola’s denial, Olivia remonstrates that<br/>Viola/Cesario is her husband. The entry of the priest apparently confirms<br/>Olivia’s claims and corroborates the ‘Contract of eternal bond of love’ and<br/>‘holy close of lips’ she made with Sebastian. Orsino in turn calls her a<br/>I’ll sacrifice the Lamb that I do love,<br/>To spite a Raven’s heart within a Dove. (5.1.2285-7)<br/>The metaphysical comedy is temporarily averted, though, by the arrival<br/>of Andrew who claims Toby has been bloodied by Cesario. In a punning<br/>twist he calls the Church the ‘devil incardinate’. He keeps cursing in the<br/>name of God as he confuses Viola with Sebastian. And when Toby enters<br/>the mayhem multiplies until he and Andrew leave to have their wounds<br/>Sebastian then appears and apologises to Olivia for hurting her ‘kinsman’,<br/>and reaffirms their wedding vows. Orsino is the first to respond to the shock<br/>of seeing the twins together.<br/>One face, one voice, one habit, and two persons,<br/>Antonio reinforces Orsino’s ‘natural’wonderment as if he was witnessing<br/>the increase of ‘creatures’ from sonnet 1, with a hint at the origins of biblical<br/>mythology in the reference to an ‘apple’. The apparent bifurcation of Cesario/<br/>Sebastian parodies the biblical division of male Adam from male God.<br/>A natural Perspective, that is, and is not. (5.1.2380-1)<br/>How have you made division of your self,<br/>And Sebastian expresses the logical impossibility of having a ‘Deity’ in<br/>his ‘nature’. By now the audience should realise that the answer lies in the<br/>An apple cleft in two, is not more twin<br/>Than these two creatures. (5.1.2387-9)<br/>Do I stand there? I never had a brother:<br/>As Sebastian and Viola share experiences, they realise they are from the<br/>same ‘womb’ and so share the father who died when they were thirteen.<br/>Viola reveals that she has usurped ‘masculine attire’ and is Sebastian’s sister.<br/>The significance of Sebastian’s reference to ‘tears’ (5.1.2406) and Viola’s<br/>Shakespearean awareness of the usurping function of the male-God is<br/>sustained as the drama unfolds.<br/>Nor can there be that Deity in my nature<br/>Of here, and everywhere. I had a sister,<br/>Whom the blind waves and surges have devour’d:<br/>Of charity, what kin are you to me? (5.1.2391-5)<br/>Significantly, at this dramatic moment in the play, the images called upon<br/>are from the Sonnet logic and not biblical mythology. Shakespeare has<br/>Sebastian ascribe to Nature the overview that brings about the logical<br/>conclusion where Olivia is betrothed to ‘a maid and man’. Viola’s admission<br/>that she has usurped the masculine is reflected by Sebastian in the Sonnet<br/>based awareness that as a male he has masculine and feminine characteristics<br/>in both body and mind.<br/>So comes it Lady, you have been mistook:<br/>Orsino confirms the force of the Sonnet logic when he gains from ‘this<br/>most happy wrack’ a ‘Boy’ (sonnet 126), who is more mature than he. He<br/>acknowledges Viola’s Feste-like understanding of the dynamic of truth by<br/>reflecting on her insistence he should never love a woman like her. Orsino’s<br/>education in the logic of truth and beauty nears completion.<br/>But Nature to her bias drew in that.<br/>You would have been contracted to a Maid,<br/>Nor are you therein (by my life) deceiv’d,<br/>You are betrothed both to a maid and man. (5.1.2425-9)<br/>I shall have share in this most happy wrack,<br/>Viola’s response is right out of the Mistress sonnets, which articulate the<br/>logic of truth. Sonnet 137 logically distinguishes between beauty as ‘seeing’<br/>and truth and ‘saying’, and sonnet 138 uses the idea of swearing as the most<br/>decided form of saying to elucidate the relation between speaking well and<br/>lying. Again Viola refers to the Earth and Sun or Nature rather than to<br/>Boy, thou hast said to me a thousand times,<br/>Thou never should’st love women like to me. (5.1.2432-4)<br/>And all those sayings, will I over swear,<br/>Orsino asks for Viola’s ‘hand’, and looks forward to seeing her in<br/>‘woman’s weeds’. The topic of her clothing raises the question of Malvolio’s<br/>whereabouts and incites Olivia to again associate their idealising<br/>psychologies. As Orsino awakens to the deeper implications of his natural<br/>logic he sees more clearly the reason for his previous attraction to the<br/>psychology of ‘dead love’.<br/>And all those swearings keep as true in soul,<br/>As doth that Orbed Continent, the fire,<br/>That severs day from night. (5.1.2435-8)<br/>They say poor Gentleman, he’s much distract.<br/>Feste makes the issue even clearer when he tells Olivia of Malvolio’s<br/>‘letter’ and says ‘madman’s Epistles are no Gospels’. Look then to be well edified, when the Fool<br/>Malvolio’s self-delusional stupidity is taken to new heights in his letter.<br/>He blames Olivia for his misguided performance in the previous scenes and<br/>he ignores his own exhibition of conceitedness before he spied the letter<br/>on the garden path. Because Malvolio’s madness is not a physiological disease<br/>but a psychological state allied to Olivia’s, Orsino acknowledges that he is<br/>not ‘distracted’. How now, art thou mad?<br/>Feste. No Madam, I do but read madness: and your<br/>Ladyship will have it as it ought to be, you must allow<br/>Olivia. Prithee read i’thy right wits.<br/>Feste. So I do Madonna: but to read his right wits, is to<br/>read thus: therefore, perpend my Princess, and give<br/>Orsino then turns to Viola to relinquish his role as ‘Master’ and accept<br/>Viola as the ‘Master’s Mistress’. The shift, which acknowledges Orsino’s<br/>appreciation of Viola’s superior judgment, elevates her to the logical role<br/>of the Mistress in the Sonnets (who is prior to the Master Mistress of the<br/>youth sequence).
